WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: What to Do When the January Indicator Says 'Sell'
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: What to Do When the January Indicator Says 'Sell'
By Mark Hulbert
996 words
1 February 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The year-to-date decline in stocks points to a rough year for equities, according to the so-called January
indicator, which is based on the historical tendency for the market's direction in January to set the tone for the
next 11 months.
Given that January was a solidly down month for the market, with the S&P 500 falling 3.6% and the Dow
Jones Industrial Average 5.3%, followers of the indicator may be considering whether to unload some of their
stocks or replace some of their riskiest bets with more-conservative holdings -- such as stocks of the largest
companies that are trading for relatively low price/book ratios, a common measure of net worth.
The January indicator, also called the January barometer, traces back to at least 1972, when newsletter
publisher Yale Hirsch first mentioned it in his annual Stock Trader's Almanac. The indicator has continued to
work just as well as it did before, which is in itself noteworthy. It is more common for stock-market patterns to
stop working once they are discovered and lots of investors begin to pay attention to them.
Consider: In the years since 1973 in which the S&P 500 rose during January, the index proceeded over the
next 11 months to gain an average of 11.2%. That compares to an average February-through-December gain
of just 0.2% in those years in which -- like this year -- the market fell during January.
"Compared to other market-timing indicators that are out there, this one appears to be one of the most
accurate," says Michael Cooper, a finance professor at the University of Utah who has studied the January
indicator.
Given that, shouldn't you immediately dump all your stocks? Mr. Cooper says no, for two reasons.
The first: Though the odds of an up market this year now are lower than they would have been had stocks
risen in January, those odds still slightly favor the market rising from now until the end of this year.
According to Sam Stovall, chief equity strategist at S&P Capital IQ, the S&P 500 since 1945 has risen 56% of
the time following down Januarys. That is lower than the 84% frequency of February-through-December
gains following a higher market in January, but still positive.
The second reason it would be unwise to dump all your stocks right now, according to Mr. Cooper, is the lack
of any plausible explanation for why the January indicator should work. He says he and other researchers
have tested a number of theories and found each of them wanting.
The absence of a convincing explanation "raises serious questions about whether it will continue working in
the future," Mr. Cooper says, since without many more years of data, it always is possible that "by sheer
coincidence" a meaningless indicator will continue working -- for a while -- even after its discovery.
Upon balancing these considerations with the January indicator's track record, Mr. Cooper says that, given
this January's performance, we might want to reduce our portfolio risk but not give up on stocks entirely.
For example, if you own stocks on margin -- borrowing from your broker to buy more than you otherwise
could -- then he suggests selling enough of your stocks to eliminate that debt. And if you are particularly risk
averse, you should build up some cash in your equity portfolios.
There are several alternatives to common stocks that are currently recommended by several of the advisers
tracked by the Hulbert Financial Digest who have beaten a broad stock-market index over the past 15 years.
One is bond mutual funds, though these advisers are favoring funds with shorter maturities, in order to reduce
their vulnerability to the higher rates that are the likely consequence of the Fed continuing to taper its
monetary stimulus program.

--- Page 38 ---
Page 38 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Their two most recommended bond funds are the Fidelity Floating Rate High Income Fund, with a 0.7%
expense ratio, or $70 per $10,000 invested; and the Vanguard Short-Term Investment-Grade Fund, with a
0.2% expense ratio. The first invests in floating-rate bank loans, while the second holds investment-grade
short-term debt.
Another category of alternatives is preferred stocks, which typically behave more like bonds than common
stocks. The preferred stock the top performers currently like most is the 6.75% preferred C shares of AES
Corp ., which generates and distributes electricity.
Two other categories popular among these market-beating advisers contain more-volatile investments. One
is master limited partnerships, which trade like stocks but return much of their gain to investors as income,
though the advisers acknowledge that some MLPs are highly leveraged and quite risky. Their most highly
recommended MLP currently is Kinder Morgan Energy Partners . The other category is gold and other
precious metals. The advisers particularly like Freeport-McMoRan Copper & Gold , a mining company.
Mr. Cooper points out that it also might be a good idea to reduce risk among the stocks you continue to own.
For example, you could favor large-cap value stocks -- those trading at relatively low price/book ratios. His
research found that a down January translates into particular rough going for stocks at the opposite end of
the spectrum -- small-cap growth ones.
Several large-cap-value stocks currently are quite popular among the advisers who have beaten the market
over the long term. Each stock sports a market cap in excess of $100 billion and a price/book ratio below that
of the S&P 500, which currently stands at 2.5.
They include telecom company AT&T ; oil firm Chevron ; computer-equipment maker Cisco Systems ;
industrial conglomerate General Electric ; chip maker Intel ; and banking giant Wells Fargo .

Stocks rebounded from a string of declines sparked by turmoil in emerging markets, getting a lift from

The Nasdaq Composite Index climbed 71.69 points, or 1.8%, to 4123.13, its largest one-day advance since

U.S. stocks have been under pressure in recent days, swept up in a rush of cash fleeing emerging nations.
On Wednesday, stocks and other riskier investments were hit hard as political and economic troubles in
developing economies such as Turkey were magnified by the Federal Reserve's decision to step up the pace
at which it pares back its stimulus efforts.
But Thursday's session saw the Dow industrials post gains for just the second time in eight sessions. The

Shares of health-care and consumer-discretionary companies led all 10 of the S&P 500's sectors higher.
Gains in U.S. stocks came as emerging-market currencies recovered from earlier losses on Thursday.
The Turkish lira and South African rand bounced back from earlier lows, as did the Russian ruble, which
Thursday slumped near a five-year low against the dollar.
The dollar edged up against the euro and the yen.
Recent efforts by emerging-market central banks to stem capital outflows have had little effect, and the Fed's
move on Wednesday to reduce bond purchases had put additional pressure on the battered currencies.
The iShares MSCI Emerging Markets stock exchange-traded fund gained 0.9% in U.S. trading, after falling to
a five-month low on Wednesday.

World News: Argentina Sheds Currency Reserves As Peso Faces Renewed Pressure
World News: Argentina Sheds Currency Reserves As Peso Faces Renewed Pressure
By Ken Parks
425 words
31 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A9
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BUENOS AIRES -- Argentina's central bank has shed $1.25 billion of its dwindling foreign-currency reserves
since it devalued the peso last week, even as the currency came under renewed pressure on Thursday.
That could imperil President Cristina Kirchner's latest attempt to head off a recession as inflation undermines
confidence in the economy.
The central bank, which tightly managed the peso, has defended the currency at around eight per dollar
after letting it slide 15% last week, but is burning through dollars doing so, according to central-bank data
released late Wednesday.
Reserves are down $2.3 billion this year, after dropping by $4.1 billion in the fourth quarter of 2013. After
peaking at $52.6 billion in January 2011, reserves stand at just under $28.3 billion.
"Reserves are like the central bank's muscles," said Luciano Cohan, chief economist at consultancy Elypsis.
"The rate of reserve loss has been very intense in recent days and that raises questions about whether [the
bank] can maintain this new exchange rate." A central bank spokesman declined to comment.
The peso weakened about one centavo to close at 8.01 per dollar on the regulated Mae currency market on
Thursday. It firmed to 12.80 on the black market, from 12.90 the previous session, newspaper El Cronista,
which tracks black-market rates, reported. The central bank said it was a net seller of dollars in the currency
market Thursday.
The devaluation has so far failed to close the confidence-busting gap between the official and black-market
rates. The gap has narrowed in recent weeks.
The bigger the gap between black-market and regulated rates, the more the Argentine public fears the official
exchange rate is overvalued and vulnerable to another devaluation.
Another measure aimed at deflating the black market also appears to be falling flat. Starting Monday,
Argentines were allowed to buy limited amounts of dollars for savings for the first time in about 18 months.
The tax agency said it had authorized purchases of more than $137 million in dollars through Thursday
afternoon. However, Argentines opted to pay a 20% tax and withdraw $125 million from the banks instead of
taking advantage of attractive interest rates and tax breaks for dollar deposits, the tax agency said.
The Kirchner administration has borrowed more than $29 billion since 2010 from the central bank to pay its
creditors, while government fuel imports and falling gold prices have also dented reserves.

Gold Declines as Fortunes Turn for Stocks, Dollar

Gold Declines as Fortunes Turn for Stocks, Dollar
By Matt Day
426 words
31 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold slid on Thursday amid stability in stock markets and a stronger dollar.
Gold for April delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell $19.70, or 1.6%, to settle at $1,242.50 a troy
ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange .
Gold had pushed to a near-nine-week high on Wednesday as renewed turmoil in emerging markets sent
global stock markets into retreat.
But Thursday data showing U.S. growth accelerated late last year, along with the Federal Reserve's decision
to hold to its stimulus rollback, sapped investor interest in gold.
"You saw that safe-haven demand coming back into gold [recently], but the markets have a bit of a reprieve
from those concerns," said Dave Meger, director of metals with Vision Financial Markets.
The Federal Reserve said after the close of regular gold trading late Wednesday it would continue to cut the
pace of its bond purchases. The Fed's bond-buying effort had been a key support for gold prices in recent
years as investors sought the metal as a hedge against the inflation that could follow the central bank's
stimulus.
Gold also was pressured as the dollar gained after the Fed's decision, and economic data showed the U.S.
economy remains on relatively firm footing. The U.S. economy expanded at a steady clip in the fourth quarter,
growing at a seasonally adjusted rate of 3.2%, the Commerce Department said Thursday.
Gold and the dollar tend to move inversely. A weaker greenback makes dollar-denominated gold futures
appear cheaper for buyers using other currencies, and some traders use gold as a hedge against a declining
dollar.
Meanwhile, platinum slumped on Thursday, tracking gold prices despite the rejection of an offer that would
have ended a strike limiting production from the top producer. South Africa's platinum mine workers rejected
a fresh wage offer from mining companies on Thursday, vowing to continue a strike that brought the sector to
a standstill since it began late last week.
Platinum for April delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell 1.5% to $1,387.60 a troy ounce on the
Nymex.
"There have been positive developments" in the standoff between the Association of Mineworkers and
Construction Union and government and corporate leaders, but no resolution, said Walter de Wet, an analyst
with Standard Bank . "Of course, if AMCU accepts the wage offer from mines tomorrow, [platinum] will be
vulnerable" to further losses.

U.S. and Asian stocks fell sharply, part of a global selloff of risky assets that deepened after the Federal
Reserve said it would cut back further on its stimulus measures.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slid 189.77 points, or 1.2%, to 15738.79. The S&P 500 gave up 18.30
points, or 1%, to 1774.20, and the Nasdaq Composite Index fell 46.53 points, or 1.1%, to 4051.43.
In Asia early Thursday, Japanese stocks suffered steep losses, with the Nikkei stock average down more
than 3% near the midpoint of the session. Elsewhere, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 1.8%, Hong
Kong's Hang Seng index was down 1.4% and South Korea's Kospi was off 1.3%.
After Wednesday's decline, the Dow has fallen for six of the past seven trading sessions and is 5.1% off its
record hit on Dec. 31.
The central bank on Wednesday said it would cut back on its monthly bond purchases by $10 billion, a
widely expected move, as officials have said the economy looks strong enough to expand on its own.
In addition to the Fed, emerging-market turmoil was the focus among many money managers. Interest-rate
increases in Turkey and South Africa failed to stanch the bleeding in those currencies, and the Fed's move
strengthened the dollar against most currencies. Both factors heightened investor jitters.
"The question is, how contagious are [losses in] emerging markets to the U.S. market?" said Jurrien Timmer ,
director of global macro strategy and portfolio manager with Fidelity Investments . He doesn't expect the
emerging-markets rout to cause long-term trouble for the U.S., though. "This is a little bit more of a regional
situation," he said. "But it scares people."
The iShares MSCI Emerging Markets fund, meant to track emerging-market stocks, declined 1.4%.
While U.S. indexes have moved lower this week, investors and traders said the declines indicated investors
broadly cutting back on risk in their portfolios, rather than bailing out of stocks.
"We haven't had this kind of volatility for a while, so I would have thought you'd have a lot more fast-money
trades," said Viren Chandrasoma, managing director in equity trading at Credit Suisse Group . Instead, he
said, the selling was steady and broad-based, as much of the emerging-market tumult was "happening
outside of the market here, and people are trying to figure out what this all means."
"We were a little extended," said John Lynch , Charlotte, N.C.-based regional chief investment officer of Wells
Fargo Private Bank, which manages $170 billion. "It could have been anything, at this point," that could spark
declines.
While he wasn't selling U.S. stocks, he wasn't ready to put more cash into the market just yet. "We'll see how
this shakes out," he said. "If we do get the elusive 10% [pullback] and we get better guidance" about
corporate earnings, "maybe that'll be a better opportunity."
In its statement, the Fed didn't mention the turbulence in emerging markets, leading some analysts to predict
that routs in the most vulnerable currencies would continue.
"From the point of view of [emerging markets], the Fed has just said 'hasta la vista, baby,'" wrote Steven
Englander, the global head of G-10 foreign-exchange strategy at CitiFX. Given that, he expects the yen,
Swiss franc and euro to do well, and "commodity currencies and [emerging markets] to do poorly."

Gold prices climbed as turmoil in emerging markets and a swoon in global stocks renewed investor interest in

Prices for the precious metal are up 5.6% from the three-year nadir reached on Dec. 19. Recent economic

gripped developing economies from Turkey to India.
The gains follow months of punishment for gold bulls. Prices fell 28% in 2013, snapping a 12-year winning
streak and making the precious metal one of the worst-performing assets of the year.
Gold for February delivery, the most actively traded contract, rose $11.40, or 0.9%, to $1,262.20 a troy ounce
Wednesday on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange , a shade below the nine-week high
reached on Friday.
Worries about the health of emerging markets intensified Wednesday after interest-rate increases by central
banks in Turkey and South Africa weren't enough to calm worries about the stability of emerging markets.
Global stock markets fell as investors moved to the Japanese yen, U.S. government debt and gold.
"If you believe that where there's smoke, there's fire, these emerging-markets issues could have far-reaching
implications" for gold, said Roy Friedman, an executive vice president with Dillon Gage Metals, a
precious-metals dealer. If the emerging-market woes linger, "we could see a continuation of money rotating
out of equities and finding a home in precious metals."
Gold had been out of favor with investors in recent months amid anticipation that the stronger global
economy would push the Federal Reserve to curb its stimulus efforts. The central bank's bond-buying
programs and other measures to boost the U.S. economy had prompted many investors to buy gold as a
hedge against inflation and other perceived risks.
But the latest step in the Fed's rollback didn't faze gold bulls. The Fed's policy committee said Wednesday
that it would cut its bond purchases to $65 billion in February, from a monthly pace of $75 billion in January.
The central bank signaled further reductions were likely in coming months.
Gold prices initially trimmed gains after the metals market had closed following the Fed's statement but
quickly recovered.
Investors had largely accounted for the central bank's move during gold's long decline, said Scott Carter ,
chief executive of Lear Capital, a precious-metals dealer. The Fed "did a good job of getting the message out
there the last couple of months that they were going to do this," Mr. Carter said.

Global Finance: RBS Curtails Use of Forex Benchmarks as Probes Widen
Global Finance: RBS Curtails Use of Forex Benchmarks as Probes Widen
By Katie Martin
484 words
29 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC has told clients it is cutting back on some currencies
benchmarks, a move that comes amid a series of global probes into possible manipulation of a range of
so-called fixes, including those for interest rates, currencies, gold and silver.
According to a memo sent to clients and seen by The Wall Street Journal, the bank -- the seventh-largest
currencies dealer by market share -- plans to stop accepting orders from clients on certain of its own
currencies fixes next month. RBS decided to pull out of these benchmarks following a review of its business
processes, including its foreign-exchange fixes, the memo said.
That decision underscores how banks are reconsidering their role in setting benchmarks against the
backdrop of increased regulatory scrutiny. The RBS memo comes days after
Deutsche Bank AG said it plans to pull out of the process for setting the fix for gold and silver. The German
lender said the move was part of a wider scaling back of its commodities business.
Regulators in the U.K. and Germany have both said they are looking into how gold fixes are determined.
Currencies benchmarks are snapshots of traded rates used by investors and asset managers to track the
values of their assets and liabilities. Many investors place currencies orders with banks to complete trades at
fix rates.
As the Journal has previously reported, RBS is one of several banks that has suspended staff amid a global
investigation into possible manipulation of the foreign-exchange markets.
According to people familiar with the matter, authorities in the U.K., U.S. and elsewhere have partly focused
their probe into currencies markets on the so-called 4 p.m. London fix, a particularly popular benchmark.
Banks have found electronic communications among traders at different banks that appear to show efforts to
collude in order to push the fix unfairly in their favor by, for example, agreeing on sequences for when
competing dealers place trades, according to people familiar with the communications.
In the memo to clients, RBS said that its traders may continue to sometimes trade around fixes. "It is
important for you to be aware that in order for RBS to appropriately manage market risk, we may enter the
market ahead of the fix. This, or other market factors, may result in the market moving for or against you
depending on the size of your order relative to market liquidity," the memo said.
The bank will continue to participate in benchmark fixes at 9 a.m. and 4 p.m. London time and 4 p.m. New
York time; the European Central Bank 1:15 p.m. fix, and benchmarks for the Indian rupee, the Korean won
and the Thai baht. RBS didn't specify which other fixes it will cease to support.

Traders Dither -- And Stocks Follow
Monday's Markets
Traders Dither -- And Stocks Follow
By Dan Strumpf
626 words
28 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks notched another loss as investors weighed whether a selloff in emerging markets that pummeled
stocks last week had run its course.
One session after the worst week for U.S. equities in more than two years, U.S. stocks spent the day
swinging between gains and losses. While global markets showed signs of stability after last week's steep
slide in emerging-market stocks and currencies, many investors appeared content to stick to the sidelines
until the dust is settled.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 41.23 points, or 0.3%, to 15837.88, its fifth losing session in a row. The
Dow has shed 621 points, or 3.8%, over the past five sessions and last week saw its biggest weekly drop
since September 2011.
The S&P 500 fell 8.73 points, or 0.5%, to 1781.56, while the Nasdaq Composite Index shed 44.56 points, or
1.1%, to 4083.61.
Most sectors ended the day lower, though utilities and telecommunications stocks -- typically defensive
corners of the market -- finished higher. The Dow found some support from Caterpillar Inc . shares, which
rallied $5.12, or 5.9%, to $91.29 after the Dow component reported better-than-expected fourth-quarter
earnings.
David Seaburg, head of equity sales trading at Cowen, said investors on Monday were "digesting" last week's
selloff. "I don't think people are in a mad rush to get back in after seeing the market trade down 300 points"
on Friday, he said.
Weak data out of China last week helped spark a selloff in emerging-market stocks and currencies, which in
turn triggered a move out of riskier investments globally. Investments viewed as havens, such as Treasurys
and gold, spiked. On Monday, investors were reassured after Turkey's central bank said it would hold an
emergency meeting and weigh higher interest rates. That sent the Turkish lira sharply higher after yet
another record-breaking slide, and helped stanch selloffs in other battered markets.
Even after last week's drop in U.S. stocks, many investors say they don't expect to see protracted declines.
They point to a U.S. economy that appears on decent footing and fourth-quarter earnings that have largely
met forecasts. But some investors say a pullback in U.S. stocks is long overdue after last year's gains. The
S&P 500 is up 18.8% from where it stood 12 months ago, but is down 3.6% from its most recent record high
hit Jan. 15.
The decline in stocks last week puts additional focus on this week's Federal Reserve policy meeting,
investors said. Fed officials will consider whether to continue winding down their stimulus measures.
"In terms of equity markets, this week all eyes are on the central banks," said Rebecca Patterson, chief
investment officer at Bessemer Trust, which manages about $60 billion. Ms. Patterson added that "nothing
fundamental for me has changed," and said she expects the central bank to announce a further withdrawal
of its stimulus program. Ms. Patterson said she favored U.S. stocks at the moment and would consider
buying if stocks pull back further.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.766% as prices fell. Gold futures slipped 0.1% to $1263.60
an ounce. March crude-oil futures declined 1% to $95.72 a barrel.
Stocks in Australia fell early Tuesday, as the market reopened after a holiday, while Japan and South Korea
stabilized after several brutal sessions.

Big Drop For Dow And S&P
Friday's Markets
Big Drop For Dow And S&P
By Matt Jarzemsky
655 words
25 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow industrials took their biggest daily tumble in more than seven months, caught up in a two-day selloff
in emerging-market stocks and currencies.
Traders said that despite the scope of Friday's decline, mutual-fund managers and other long-term investors
weren't overly urgent in selling.
Rather, they pointed to short-term participants selling baskets of stocks, such as exchange-traded funds, as a
way to protect against losses elsewhere in their portfolios. Buyers, meanwhile, pulled to the sidelines, content
to wait before putting money to work.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slid 318.24 points, or 2%, to 15879.11, a more-than five-week low. For the
week, the benchmark fell 3.5%, its biggest weekly slide since November 2011.
The S&P 500 index dropped 38.17 points, or 2.1%, to 1790.29. For the week, the index fell 2.6%, its biggest
weekly decline since June 2012. The Russell 2000 index of smaller companies dropped 2.4% on Friday, its
biggest one-day percentage decline since June.
But even with Friday's declines, the S&P 500 is up 19% from a year ago and 3.1% off its Jan. 15 all-time
closing high.
The selloff has come as U.S. stocks were swamped by the downdraft in emerging-market stocks and
currencies, traders said.
Unexpectedly bad economic news out of China, coupled with growing political and fiscal uncertainty in a
number of other developing nations has sent many emerging markets into a tailspin. This turn of events
blindsided U.S. investors, who have been mainly focused on the outlook for Federal Reserve policy and
corporate earnings, traders said.
"I don't think the market was positioned for the recent events." said Doug Crofton, head of U.S. cash equity
trading at Bank of America Merrill Lynch .
Still, "we're not seeing people throw in the towel" on U.S. stocks, Mr. Crofton said.
While the downdraft hasn't sent many longer-term stock investors to the exits, investors say they are looking
at stocks in a different light.
Whereas last year's rally had many investors buying stocks on even the slightest pullback, many seem
content to be sitting this pullback out for now.
In part that is because, thanks to last year's big gains, valuations in many corners of the market aren't as
appealing, many investors say. At the same time, some see the Fed paring back its easing efforts as a
potential headwind.
"The response I'm getting from people who generally were 'buy on the dip'-type accounts is we're looking at
things a little differently now," said David Seaburg, head of sales trading at New York brokerage Cowen & Co.
In addition, many investors had come into 2014 focusing on stocks that would benefit from an upswing in
global economic growth. Emerging-market troubles are causing some to rethink that approach.
"The companies that have the most exposure to the emerging world are going to feel it, and we'll see in
revenues and earnings," says Jack Ablin , chief investment officer at BMO Private Bank, which oversees $66
billion.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Assets seen as havens continued to benefit. Gold futures rose 0.2% to settle at $1,264.50 an ounce, a
two-month high.
Buying in U.S. government bonds pushed the 10-year Treasury note's yield down to 2.737%, below its
seven-week low of 2.774% on Thursday. Treasury yields fall as prices rise.
In the corporate arena Friday, Microsoft rose 75 cents, or 2.1%, to $36.81 after reporting
better-than-expected quarterly earnings and revenue, helped by strong demand for the company's new Xbox
videogame console.
Kansas City Southern tumbled 17.79, or 15%, to 99.49 after the railroad operator reported a
smaller-than-expected increase in fourth-quarter earnings.

Reckoning in Caracas
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Reckoning in Caracas
376 words
24 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
One of Margaret Thatcher's many great lines was that the problem with socialism is that eventually you run
out of other people's money. Welcome to Venezuela.
That country's bonds took a dive Wednesday on news of a partial devaluation of the bolivar. Economy
vice-president Rafael Ramirez announced that the central bank would no longer sell dollars to airlines or
Venezuelan travelers at the official exchange rate of 6.3 bolivars. Instead those buyers of dollars will pay
11.36 bolivars.
That's a big devaluation, but with the black market rate at more than 70 bolivars to the dollar, a much larger
and broader adjustment is inevitable. Venezuela is facing a classic balance-of-payments crisis, which is what
happens when a country drives out human and investment capital and then inflates the currency to paper
over the trouble.
Venezuelan oil exports once generated enough foreign exchange to pay its dollar debts. But today Venezuela
produces about the same 2.9 million barrels of oil per day as it did in 1998. Cuba gets about 100,000 barrels
a day in exchange for supplying doctors and teachers. And more than 600,000 barrels a day go to China to
pay down some $40 billion in loans that the late Hugo Chavez took out to win elections.
Public spending has boomed, and Chavez and successor Nicolas Maduro have treated the state-owned oil
company like their personal cash machine. In 2012 PdVSA paid $20 billion in taxes, $9 billion to government
social programs and another $8.5 billion to a state development fund. Venezuela is now running a fiscal
deficit of between 12% and 15% of GDP and the central bank keeps printing bolivars to pay local bills. Even
with price controls, the government says 2013 inflation was 56%.
The central bank holds $20.5 billion in reserves, down from $28 billion a year ago. More than half are in gold
and thus not liquid. A large devaluation coupled with price controls will drive many importers out of business.
That could produce more shortages. The socialist bills are coming due in Caracas.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Roadkill on the Path To Greater Equality" -- WSJ Feb. 13, 2014)

Stocks Mixed After Profit Reports
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Mixed After Profit Reports
By Kaitlyn Kiernan
554 words
22 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks finished mixed as the latest batch of earnings news failed to give the market a fresh sense of
direction.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 44.12 points, or 0.27%, to 16414.44, after trading down as much as
142 points earlier in the day. The stock market was closed Monday for the Martin Luther King Jr. holiday.
The S&P 500 index added 5.10 points, or 0.28%, to 1843.80, with utilities and materials shares leading gains
in eight of 10 sector groups. That reversed an earlier loss of as much as six points. The Nasdaq Composite
Index gained 28.18 points, or 0.67%, to 4225.76.
Stocks started the day moving higher after China's central bank moved to calm jitters in money markets
there overnight. But at midmorning, U.S. stocks reversed direction and meandered through the rest of the
session.
With no major economic news due this week, investors are eyeing individual corporate earnings reports. But
at this stage, earnings news hasn't provided the market with a new sense of direction, traders said.
With 12% of companies reporting, the S&P 500 is on track to report aggregate fourth-quarter profit growth of
5.9% from the previous year, according to FactSet. Analysts expected 6.2% of profit growth before the
unofficial start of earnings season.
"We have to see earnings increase to cover the higher valuations that we've seen," said Bruce Bittles , chief
investment strategist at Robert W. Baird & Co .
Mr. Bittles sees the stock market eventually breaking higher but said investor optimism must first be
tempered. "When optimism is too extreme, most people are already invested and no one is ready to come in,"
he said.
Travelers and Verizon Communications led declines in the Dow after reporting results early Tuesday.
Travelers declined $1.47, or 1.7%, to $85 despite beating fourth-quarter earnings and revenue expectations
amid price increases and fewer claims costs a year after superstorm Sandy.
Verizon Communications dropped 65 cents, or 1.3%, to $47.70 after the company said it swung to a
fourth-quarter profit as the phone carrier continues to benefit from subscriber growth.
"You've got a sell the news reaction in the Dow," said Joe Saluzzi , co-head of equity trading at Themis
Trading . "These companies had quite the run last year, and now they're getting earnings results."
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note dropped to 2.82%.
February crude-oil futures climbed 0.7% to settle at $94.99 a barrel, while January gold futures eased 0.8%
to $1,242.30 an ounce, after settling Friday at a five-week high. The dollar lost ground against the euro and
the yen.
European markets gained, with the region's benchmark index on an early path toward a six-year high, as
China's moves helped offset a surprise decline in economic sentiment in Germany. The Stoxx Europe 600
rose 0.1%.
Early Wednesday, Asian stocks were down, with Australia leading the region lower after news of higher than
expected inflation. The S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.6%. Japan's Nikkei was was off 0.1% before the central
bank announced the results of its policy meeting.

World News -- Europe File: Euro Zone vs. Deflation Ogre
World News -- Europe File: Euro Zone vs. Deflation Ogre
By Simon Nixon
624 words
21 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Europe's low inflation rate has become the new focal point for those who believe the euro zone is doomed to
disaster.
Inflation in the zone fell to just 0.8% in December, well below the European Central Bank target of "close to
but below 2%." In Greece, inflation is already negative, while in Spain, Portugal and Ireland it ranges
between 0.2% and 0.3%. That is fueling fears the currency area could tip into outright deflation, as Japan did
in the 1990s when falling prices led to prolonged stagnation. Consumers held off purchases as they waited
for goods to become cheaper, causing growth to stall and the debt burdens to rise.
The latest to lend its voice to the chorus of anxiety is the International Monetary Fund , whose chief, Christine
Lagarde , last week warned that deflation was a rising risk for the global economy, which could be disastrous
for the recovery. "If inflation is the genie, then deflation is the ogre that must be fought decisively," she said.
There was little doubt who Ms. Lagarde thinks needs to do the fighting. Senior ECB officials have been
summoned to Washington to be presented with the IMF's gloomy forecasts and privately urged to take bolder
action.
Even if inflation in some countries does turn negative for a period, this isn't necessarily a bad thing.
In a monetary union where it is impossible to inflate away one's debts, a downward adjustment in prices may
be essential to allow economies to regain competitiveness -- a so-called internal devaluation. Switzerland has
endured frequent periods of falling prices, as did 19th century Britain. Crucially, surveys show that euro-zone
citizens expect inflation to return to 2% over the medium term.
Nonetheless, the risk of euro-zone deflation is clearly not zero -- and if the ECB were a normal central bank
answerable to a single government it might already be doing more to loosen monetary policy, including by
embarking on large-scale asset purchases.
Critics warn that by not acting now, the ECB risks repeating the policy errors of the Bank of Japan , which
didn't act swiftly enough to prevent the slide into deflation.
But this focus on the ECB misses a crucial aspect of the Japanese phenomenon. Central bankers insist that
all they can do is buy time for politicians, firms and households to make the adjustments necessary to put
their finances on a more sustainable footing.
It was not only -- or even mainly -- policy mistakes by the BOJ that held Japan back for two decades; it was
Japan's failure to deliver the measures needed to allow the economy to rebalance.
It took Japan more than a decade after the bubble burst to recapitalize its banking system, during which time
the economy was starved of credit. Japanese labor laws and working practices are inflexible, creating a
two-tier workforce that condemns many younger workers to temporary contracts while older workers enjoy
gold-plated permanent contracts.
Despite a falling population and rapidly aging society, Japan remained resistant to immigration while female
participation in the workforce is very low and provision for child care poor. Worse, the Japanese public and
private sectors suffer from endemic cronyism, with powerful vested interests able to resist any attack on their
privileges.
In the U.K., Ireland, Spain and Portugal, there are signs that decisive action is starting to pay off. But the
reality is that much of Europe, like Japan, remains hamstrung by cronyism, corruption and vested interests. It
is this -- far more than the ECB's reluctance to buy bonds -- that poses the greatest long-term risk to
euro-zone growth and prospects for deflation.

Earnings Reports From Intel, GE Weigh on Stocks
Friday's Markets
Earnings Reports From Intel, GE Weigh on Stocks
By Matt Jarzemsky
1,433 words
18 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks mostly fell ahead of the holiday weekend, as corporate heavyweights such as Intel and General
Electric slumped on earnings news.
The S&P 500 sank 7.19 points, or 0.4%, to 1838.70, about 10 points shy of Wednesday's record. The
Nasdaq Composite Index shed 21.11, or 0.5%, to 4197.58.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average bucked the retreat, rising 41.55 points, or 0.3%, to 16458.56, bolstered by
gains in Visa, International Business Machines and Goldman Sachs Group . Those stocks' relatively high
prices and a disproportionate impact on the price-weighted Dow helped the blue-chip index gain versus other
market benchmarks.
For the week, major benchmarks were little changed, with the Dow up 0.1%. U.S. stock trading will be closed
Monday for the Martin Luther King Jr. Day holiday.
Traders said investors have hesitated to place big bets on the broader market's next move, noting the
Chicago Board Options Exchange Volatility Index, a measure of options traders' expectation of future price
swings, remains near historic lows. But a number of single stocks have seen wild swings the past few days,
amid the first big wave of fourth-quarter earnings reports.
"The trading dynamic has been characterized much more by single-stock volatility driven by news items than
by just overall market volatility," said Joe Spinelli, head of North America cash equity trading at Deutsche
Bank AG .
On Monday, Lululemon Athletica 's shares tumbled 17% after the yoga-gear maker warned of
weaker-than-expected quarterly sales. Best Buy slid 29% Thursday after reporting sluggish holiday sales, and
the retailer fell another $2.40, or 8.9%, to $24.43 Friday.
Earnings-related stock moves Friday included a 69-cent, or 2.6%, drop to 25.85 in Intel shares after a mixed
report that analysts said damped investor enthusiasm. GE fell 62 cents, or 2.3%, to 26.58 after missing a key
profit-margin target.
Morgan Stanley rose 1.40, or 4.4%, to 33.40 after a better-than-expected quarterly profit report.
"We've gotten into the part of the year where people may be less focused on macro" events such as
economic data and global central-bank policy "and more focused on micro" themes such as profits at
specific companies, said Josh Chisari, who helps oversee about $9 billion as a client portfolio manager at J.P.
Morgan 's Intrepid Funds.
Corporate profits may need to live up to expectations for stocks' rally to continue. The S&P 500's 30%
advance last year was driven more by upbeat sentiment than improved business fundamentals, leaving
stocks more richly valued relative to corporate earnings than they were a year ago.
Of the 53 companies in the S&P 500 that have posted earnings for the latest quarter, 57% have topped
analysts' average earnings estimate, according to FactSet. But many are beating relatively low expectations.
Ninety-six companies have warned that fourth-quarter results would be worse than Wall Street projected,
compared to just 15 projecting better-than-expected results.
In all, analysts expect S&P 500 companies' latest-quarter earnings rose 5.9% from a year earlier, including
actual results for companies that already reported.
In other markets, buying in 10-year Treasury notes pushed their yield down to 2.829%, from 2.843% late
Thursday.

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Crude oil gained 0.4% to $94.37 a barrel, while gold added 0.9% to $1,251.70 a troy ounce.
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 tacked on 0.6% amid upbeat U.K. retail-sales data.
Asian markets were mostly lower. China's Shanghai Composite lost 0.9% to close at its lowest since last July.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average eased 0.1%.
In corporate news, Capital One Financial fell 4.05, or 5.3%, to 72.39 after reporting disappointing earnings
late Thursday.
United Parcel Service fell 58 cents, or 0.6%, to 99.91 after cutting its 2013 profit forecast due to a
Christmas-shopping surge catching the shipping company off guard.
American Express advanced 3.19, or 3.6%, to 90.97 after reporting that its profit more than doubled from a
year ago, boosted by growth in spending by credit-card customers.

The Intelligent Investor: New Warnings From An Investing Old Master
The Intelligent Investor: New Warnings From An Investing Old Master
By Jason Zweig
867 words
18 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Nowhere to run, nowhere to hide -- and no one to get unbiased advice from.
Judging by my inbox, that is how a lot of investors feel. U.S. and most international stocks, bonds and real
estate are all at least moderately overpriced by historical standards; cash offers a negative return after
inflation; and most market pundits have a vested interest in their advice.
For some forthright suggestions on how investors should think about today's markets, I turned to investing
pioneer Dean LeBaron, one of the most original and open-minded financial thinkers I know of. His motto has
long been: "Look for the questions that are not being asked."
He suggests following the money -- the Chinese money, that is -- and investing in Africa, Canada and Mexico.
He also likes assets that could benefit if the Federal Reserve's low-interest-rate policy backfires, such as gold
bullion, raw land and inflation-protected bonds.
What makes Mr. LeBaron worth listening to?
As he likes to say, "If the choice is between being first and being best, being first is better." Mr. LeBaron
founded Batterymarch Financial Management in Boston in 1969, investing mainly in small-company stocks,
an opportunity almost nobody else was yet interested in. Batterymarch also pioneered quantitative investing,
which uses statistical analysis -- not human judgment -- to pick stocks. In the 1970s, he says, every employee
at the firm had three computers: "We had 90 machines when many firms didn't have one."
In 1974, Batterymarch became one of the earliest firms to offer an index fund, the kind of autopilot portfolio
later popularized by Vanguard Group. In 1978, long before most investors ventured overseas, Batterymarch
began investing internationally; in 1987, the firm started investing in "Third World countries," now called
emerging markets.
Mr. LeBaron also invested in farmland decades before most asset managers did; he was among the first to
put money into privatizations as Russia divested state-owned companies.
Mr. LeBaron sold Batterymarch to Legg Mason in 1995 for almost $60 million. He retired about a year later,
but he certainly isn't retiring. As unorthodox as ever, Mr. LeBaron, 80 years old, spoke to me this past week
from his home near Sarasota, Fla.
For decades, the name of the game for investors has been to make as much money as possible. From now
on, Mr. LeBaron thinks, the prime directive will be to "lose as little money as possible."
He warns, "If we are in a transition period, then the person who is in the most danger is the one who has
recently done well, because he's done well on things that are about to change."
In Mr. LeBaron's view, the easy-money policies of central banks, including the Fed, have created what he
calls "administrative markets" -- in which prices are set at least partly by government policy rather than by
market forces.
But, he worries, that can't last forever. "In complex systems, the dynamics are predictable but the timing isn't,"
he says. "It's like adding a grain of sand one at a time to a pile: You can't tell when it will collapse, but you
know it will."
Mr. LeBaron, who invests only his own money nowadays, has no exposure to the U.S. stock market; the only
bonds he owns are inflation-protected U.S. Treasurys.
The only stocks he owns are based in China, among them INESA Electron , a technology manufacturer, and
Shanghai Chlor-Alkali Chemical . He holds the shares directly through certificates in safe-deposit boxes,

--- Page 62 ---
Page 62 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
rather than in electronic form; he thinks it is risky to have "custodial" banks hold electronic securities for
safekeeping.
Mr. LeBaron believes the Chinese have more fiscal and monetary discipline than the U.S. So, he says, "I
follow China around the world and invest where they invest." Recently that has meant countries like Nigeria,
Ghana and Kenya, as well as Canada and Mexico. Individual investors with the same idea might consider
diversified, low-cost funds like the Market Vectors Africa Index exchange-traded fund, which charges 0.8% in
annual expenses, or $80 per $10,000 invested, and the iShares MSCI Canada and iShares MSCI Mexico
Capped ETFs, costing 0.53% apiece.
Can individual investors still succeed in the riskier world he foresees? Absolutely, Mr. LeBaron says.
"Institutions tend to invest with a one- to three-year time horizon at most," he says. "My suggestion for
individuals is to change the horizon. Go longer."
Or go gutsier. If you are tough, you might try emulating one of Mr. LeBaron's most successful techniques at
Batterymarch, where he ran a contest to see who could pick the stocks that would perform worst -- not best --
over the next year.
"It's harder than it sounds," he says -- especially when you realize that Mr. LeBaron then went out and bought
them all, more than 100 at a time. If you can hold on for several years, he says, "you should make enough on
the ones that don't go bankrupt to make up for the ones that do."

Stocks Afflicted by Earnings Flu --- Weak Corporate Reports From Citigroup , Best Buy , Goldman Pressure Shares; 'The Market Is on Pause'
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Afflicted by Earnings Flu --- Weak Corporate Reports From Citigroup , Best Buy , Goldman
Pressure Shares; 'The Market Is on Pause'
By Kaitlyn Kiernan
640 words
17 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Disappointing corporate earnings pushed stocks mostly lower, pulling the S&P 500 back from a record.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 64.93 points, or 0.4%, to 16417.01, as UnitedHealth and Goldman
Sachs Group paced a decline in 19 of its 30 components.
The S&P 500 dropped 2.49 points, or 0.1%, to 1845.89. The index had rallied 1.6% the previous two sessions
to close Wednesday at an all-time high. After a 30% rise in the S&P 500 last year, stocks have had a hard
time sustaining that momentum amid concerns that share prices have gotten too lofty.
"The market is on pause," said Quincy Krosby, a market strategist at Prudential Financial , which manages
roughly $1 trillion in assets. "Investors want to get through some more earnings and then decide if growth is
commensurate with valuations," she said.
Citigroup sank $2.39, or 4.3%, to $52.60, after reporting results that missed analyst estimates amid continued
weakness in mortgages and fixed-income trading.
Best Buy tumbled 10.74, or 29%, to 26.83, after reporting holiday revenue that was below year-earlier levels
amid a decline in domestic same-store sales. Ahead of the results, Best Buy had lost 5.8% this month, after
more than tripling in 2013.
Meanwhile, Dow components Goldman Sachs and UnitedHealth also dropped after reporting results, with
Goldman losing 3.58, or 2%, to 175.17, and UnitedHealth declining 2.08, or 2.8%, to 72.76.
Goldman said fourth-quarter earnings and revenue fell from a year earlier but beat analyst expectations.
UnitedHealth fell after the insurer warned of the impact of possible cuts in Medicare this year. UnitedHealth
reported results in line with estimates as fourth-quarter income improved 15% amid increasing enrollments.
"Investors are paying the closest attention to corporate results," said Jack Ablin , chief investment officer at
BMO Private Bank, which oversees $66 billion in Chicago. "As long as the economic news confirms growth,
investors are going to file that away" to focus on earnings, he said.
The Nasdaq Composite Index bucked the broader decline, gaining 3.80 points, or 0.1%, to 4218.69.
While stocks fell, the price on the 10-year Treasury note rebounded, sending yields down to 2.843% from
2.884% late Wednesday.
Crude oil slipped 0.2%, to settle at $93.96 a barrel, while gold rose 0.2%, to settle at $1,240 a troy ounce.
The dollar lost some ground against the euro and yen.
European markets also eased amid worries over weak price rises in the euro zone. The Stoxx Europe 600 fell
0.2% to pull back from a six-year high reached in the previous session. The annual rate of euro-zone
inflation in December declined to 0.8% from 0.9% in November, further below the European Central Bank 's
target of close to 2%.

Fast End To Lira as A Haven
Currency Trading
Fast End To Lira as A Haven
By Joe Parkinson and Yeliz Candemir
422 words
17 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ISTANBUL -- Two years ago, with Turkey's economy registering near double-digit growth, Prime Minister
Recep Tayyip Erdogan hailed the lira as one of the world's most stable currencies. His government unveiled a
new symbol for it: a T-shaped anchor, meant to herald the lira's status as a "safe harbor."
Now, the lira is the world's fastest-falling emerging-market currency, losing 21% of its value against the dollar
in the past 12 months. Thursday, the lira slumped as much as 0.9% to 2.21 against the dollar, its fourth
record low of 2014 in just 11 trading days.
Economists and analysts are now predicting the currency will drop much further, particularly as long as the
country's central bank remains reluctant to raise interest rates. Some Turks are returning to old habits,
selling liras to buy foreign currency and buying gold to hedge against further falls. According to the Turkish
central bank , consumers' holdings of foreign currency rose almost 10% last year to $69 billion. Turkish
companies are following suit, raising their foreign-currency deposits by more than 20% in 2013 to $50 billion.
"We're seeing aspects of a crisis mentality again now. The numbers are worrying," said Gokhan Karakan,
who has traded currencies in Istanbul's historic Grand Bazaar for over a decade.
Economists say consumers have given up hope of a rebound. "Rather than seeing the recent episode of lira
weakness as an opportunity to switch their foreign-currency holdings into liras, Turkish households have
accelerated their foreign-currency deposit buildup since July," Barclays PLC said in a year-end report.
In Istanbul, businesspeople who rode Turkey's boom in recent years say they are holding on to dollars to
hedge against the sliding lira. "As volatility and turmoil have increased, we now prefer to keep our dollars if
we don't need to sell them," says Sait Bayhan, who has sold carpets for five decades. "But I can't just make a
profit by hoarding dollars -- we need economic stability."
A corruption scandal that has rocked Turkey's government since mid-December has magnified investors'
concerns over slowing growth and lingering economic difficulties, including a current-account deficit around
7% of gross domestic product.
The lira's decline isn't due only to local factors. Currencies in emerging markets from Brazil to Thailand have
fallen in response to rising political risk and an improving growth outlook in developed markets.

Gold Bulls Put Beating Behind Them --- Bets Surge After the Precious Metal's 28% Decline in 2013
Gold Bulls Put Beating Behind Them --- Bets Surge After the Precious Metal's 28% Decline in 2013
By Gregory Zuckerman and Tatyana Shumsky
889 words
14 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After a year of carnage in the gold market, some brave investors are betting on a comeback.
Hedge funds including San Francisco's $3 billion Passport Capital have been buying the metal, which
plunged 28% in 2013. Vermillion Asset Management LLC, a commodity-focused hedge-fund firm
majority-owned by Carlyle Group LP that manages almost $1 billion, expects next month to start its first
dedicated metals fund that will include investments in gold and other metals, according to people familiar with
the firm. The launch is partly to capitalize on gold's largest annual decline in 32 years, the people say.
Bullish gold positions held by speculators such as hedge funds hit their highest level in seven weeks,
according to data from the Commodity Futures Trading Commission .
Other investors who were burned last year when gold posted its first annual price decline in 13 years say
they are holding on, convinced a sustained rebound is ahead.
Gold continues to "provide good insurance against crisis, higher taxes and inflation risks," says John
Brynjolfsson , who runs hedge fund Armored Wolf, which advises two outside funds of about $1 billion. The
funds lost 2% and 11% last year, respectively, partly due to gold holdings. Mr. Brynjolfsson cited continued
gold buying from China and instability in the Middle East as reasons to stick to his firm's gold wagers.
The bets underscore the continuing market debate over gold amid low inflation and a resilient dollar,
following a roaring 2013 stock-market rally. The precious metal often is seen as way of protecting against
high inflation and a weak dollar. Many investors fled the gold camp last year, when the Fed first signaled it
would soon start winding down its bond-purchasing program.
But gold is off to a better start than stocks so far in 2014. On Monday, gold futures for January delivery
gained $4.20, or 0.34%, to $1250.90 on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange .
Gold is up 4.08% so far in 2014. By contrast, the Dow Jones Industrial Average has dropped 1.92% this year.
Some investors likely are buying gold simply to track two of the world's most closely followed commodity
indexes, the Dow Jones UBS Commodity Index and the S&P GSCI. Both indexes are carrying out an annual
adjustment to their commodity holdings to reflect changes in production and liquidity, or ease of trading. This
year, the DJ-UBS index will raise its gold allocation to 11.53% from 10.82%, while the S&P GSCI will trim its
gold allocation to 2.80% from 2.81% last year. Funds that follow either index will need to buy gold to match
the new weights, as gold's declines eroded the value of both allocations.
The rebalancing is closely watched because about $80 billion of investor cash tracks the S&P GSCI and
about $75 billion tracks the DJ UBS as of the end of 2012, according to data from S&P Dow Jones Indices.
Cumulatively, the gold allocation of the two indexes is followed by about $1.1 billion, Ms. Gunzberg said.
Bart Melek, head of commodity strategy at TD Securities , says index rebalancing will lead to additional
purchases of the equivalent of 1.97 million ounces of gold, or about 2.2% of the annual output of gold mines.
To be sure, many investors say this year's rise is little more than a short-term bounce after a painful tumble.
Gold remains down nearly 34% since hitting a record high of $1,888.70 an ounce in August 2011.
"Even if guys are buying, most of them aren't talking about it -- nobody wants to be seen holding it," says
Jared Dillian, a former Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc . trader who now writes a financial newsletter. "That's
how bad it's gotten."
Kathleen Kelley, who runs $110 million hedge fund Queen Anne's Gate Capital Management in New York,
has used the recent rally to increase her short bets against gold. She argues that "the dollar will be very

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
strong this year" as the U.S. economy shows marked improvement, and notes that redemptions from gold
exchange-traded funds have continued in recent weeks. Demand is "just not there," she says.
Some recent bulls already are selling. Nic Johnson, a portfolio manager at the Pimco Commodity Alpha Fund,
a hedge fund managing around $100 million, purchased bullish wagers on gold when prices sank toward
$1,200 an ounce in December. He viewed gold as having become inexpensive compared with other inflation
hedges.
When prices reached $1,240 an ounce about a week ago, Mr. Johnson sold his gold wagers. "We felt that
the probability of a further rally past $1,240 was small," he said.
Still, some hedge funds and other investors have become more bullish. John Burbank, who runs Passport,
says a possible rise in Asian demand is a key factor. Passport has been buying gold futures and betting
against gold-mining companies with heavy debt, to profit from a rebound. "If Indian buyers come back and
Chinese buying keeps growing, sentiment could change," Mr. Burbank said.

Jobs Data Stymie Stock Rally
Friday's Markets
Jobs Data Stymie Stock Rally
By Matt Jarzemsky and Min Zeng
799 words
11 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
An unexpectedly weak reading on job growth sparked a rally in bonds and shouts of surprise on trading desks
but left many stock investors at an impasse.
While sentiment remains bullish on the outlook for stocks, a much weaker-than-expected rise in December
nonfarm-payroll employment raised questions about the U.S. economy's momentum just as many investors
were feeling more confident that growth was picking up. That left stock investors unsure of the market's ability
to extend its record-breaking rally in the near term.
However, with the Federal Reserve expected to keep its easy-money policies in place, especially after the
weak jobs data, investors say a serious pullback in stock prices is unlikely.
The result was that stocks ended Friday's session little changed, with the market unable to regain momentum
lost since closing 2013 at records.
The S&P 500 index rose 4.24 points, or 0.2%, to 1842.37. The Dow Jones Industrial Average lagged behind,
dropping 7.71 points, or less than 0.1%, to 16437.05.
However, bonds rallied after the Labor Department reported the U.S. added 74,000 payrolls in December.
The result fell far short of economists' median forecast of 200,000, according to a Wall Street Journal poll.
Buying in 10-year Treasury notes sent the yield 0.106 percentage points lower to 2.858%, the biggest
one-day decline since September. Trading turned frenetic right after the report, with trading volume
exceeding over $400 billion as of 2 p.m. EST, the highest since June, according to some analysts.
"It was a slingshot move," said Adrian Miller, director of fixed income at GMP Securities in New York. "There
was lots of shouting."
Exclamations of surprise also emanated from the trading floor of futures brokerage R.J. O'Brien & Associates
in Chicago, said Todd Colvin, senior vice president at the firm.
In Baltimore, Stifel Nicolaus & Co. managing director Dave Lutz said a chat room on his trading terminal
instantly turned to a "sea of red," the color of incoming messages, as market participants scrambled to make
sense of the data.
But for all the early noise, traders said the jobs report served more to keep stock investors in a holding
pattern. After a 30% rally in the S&P 500 last year, many investors say U.S. stocks are starting to look pricey.
As a result, investors say they have been more inclined to trim back holdings than put fresh money to work.
Rex Macey , chief investment officer at Wilmington Trust , which oversees $79 billion, said the report
portends a stock market that is "going to look a lot more sideways than up, but that doesn't mean it doesn't go
up."
For both bonds and stocks, there was good news, with investors saying it means the Fed is unlikely to
accelerate the pace at which is scales back the bond purchases it has been employing to help the economy.
In December, the Fed decided to trim its bond purchases by $10 billion to $75 billion. A spate of
stronger-than-expected economic news had some investors beginning to speculate that the Fed would speed
up its "tapering."
"The Fed is going to take its time" in withdrawing monetary stimulus, said Dan Mulholland, head of U.S.
Treasury trading at BNY Mellon Capital Markets .
But for stock investors, the notion that the economy has hit another pothole was reason for caution.

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"On one hand, [the weak report] is good news because the Federal Reserve will slow down its taper, on the
other hand, it's bad news because earnings aren't going to deliver" if the labor market drags on the broader
economy, said Ron Florance, a Scottsdale, Ariz.-based deputy chief investment officer at Wells Fargo Private
Bank , which oversees $170 billion.
In the corporate arena, Alcoa dropped 58 cents, or 5.4%, to $10.11 after its bottom line fell short of analysts'

In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 rose 0.5%, bolstered by better-than-expected French industrial-production
data.
Asian markets were mixed. China's Shanghai Composite shed 0.7% to the lowest close since July 31, 2013,
after disappointing trade data, while Japan's Nikkei Stock Average gained 0.2%.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager -- Upside: How Imminent Is Inflation?
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager -- Upside: How Imminent Is Inflation?
By Joe Light
954 words
11 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Many people fear that high inflation is right around the corner. The evidence says they shouldn't.
Still, there are moves investors can take that will pay off even if inflation is low and provide ample benefit if
inflation takes off.
For now, the market is sanguine -- though some high-profile individuals aren't. On Tuesday, the day after the
U.S. Senate voted to confirm Janet Yellen as chairwoman of the Federal Reserve, Sen. Richard Shelby (R.,
Ala.), who voted against the nomination, explained his opposition to the Fed's easy-money policies.
Said Sen. Shelby: "Should inflation expectations become unmoored, prices could increase uncontrollably."
Others warning about the prospects of higher inflation include Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia
President Charles Plosser .
Though the harbingers of doom are loud, the market doesn't seem to believe them. Instead, it is pricing in low
average inflation, even in the long term. Since the late 1980s, those expectations have tended to come very
close to reality. If anything, they have been too high.
Investors expect prices to rise only about 1.7% a year on average over the next decade, according to a
measure designed by Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland economist Joe Haubrich , and his colleagues
George Pennacchi and Peter Ritchken.
Mr. Haubrich's formula weighs several indicators, including the price of inflation-related investments and
surveys of forecasters. He says his method is more accurate than other yardsticks, such as the yield of
10-year Treasury inflation-protected securities, or TIPS, which are influenced by other factors.
"If a big portion of the investing public really thought we were going to have high inflation, they wouldn't put
up with interest rates at the level we see them," Mr. Haubrich says.
Investors' estimates could be optimistic, but historically, they have come much closer to where inflation
ended up than extreme forecasts, says Menzie Chinn, an economist at the University of Wisconsin in
Madison.
Investors' expectations have become more accurate over time, according to a Wall Street Journal analysis of
Mr. Haubrich's data and inflation measured by the Bureau of Labor Statistics .
In January 1982, when Mr. Haubrich's data begins, investors believed that inflation over the next decade
would average 6.2%. But by January 1992, prices had risen by only 3.9% a year.
In January 1992, investors thought inflation would be about 3.4% annually over 10 years. It ended up being
2.5%.
Between January 1982 and November 2003, investors' 10-year inflation expectations were too low in only 33
of 263 months.
To be sure, 1982 was close to the start of a three-decade-long bull market in bonds that is unlikely to repeat.
The Fed's stimulus programs also are unprecedented, which could make investors' inflation expectations
less accurate.
What does that say about today?
Since investors don't expect inflation to reach even 2% over the next decade, it could be foolish to build a
portfolio that prepares for rampant inflation. However, there are some financial moves to make that are good
ideas in and of themselves and will provide protection if prices spike.

--- Page 71 ---
Page 71 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One old idea that remains good: I Savings Bonds, which are sold directly by the federal government at
TreasuryDirect.gov. Right now, they pay a fixed interest rate of 0.2% plus an adjustable interest rate tied to
inflation, for a total annual rate of 1.38%. The inflation component of the yield adjusts every six months and
can be negative, but the overall rate never falls below zero.
The bonds can be sold with a small penalty after one year, making them an effective alternative to
money-market accounts and short-term certificates of deposit, which pay rates of 1% or less. They also are a
better deal than short-term TIPS. A five-year TIPS, for example, pays a yield of minus-0.5% before inflation.
Unfortunately, an investor can buy only $10,000 worth of the bonds per calendar year, plus an additional
$5,000 worth using a tax refund. That makes it difficult to build a substantial stake in the bonds quickly.
Stocks also generally provide some inflation protection, as rising prices should allow companies to increase
their earnings, says Gary Thayer, chief macro strategist at Wells Fargo Advisors , which manages $1.3
trillion.
Materials and energy stocks in particular tend to benefit with higher inflation. Energy stocks are relatively
cheap, with a price/earnings ratio of 13.9 based on trailing 12-month earnings, compared with 16.8 for the
S&P 500 as a whole, according to FactSet. Materials companies, with a P/E of 19.7, are expensive.
Another good move: If you already are planning to buy a home, do it soon. Over very long periods, home
prices tend to keep up with inflation.
The rate of 30-year fixed-rate mortgages, now at 4.6%, is low and expected to rise as the Federal Reserve
winds down its bond-buying stimulus program.
Since inflation may be accompanied by wage growth, while mortgage payments are fixed, a rise in prices
could make it easier for a homeowner to pay off the debt, says Keith Gumbinger , vice president at HSH.com,
a mortgage-information website.
Investors should avoid the temptation to make large bets on commodities, gold or other investments that
might benefit only if inflation spikes, Mr. Thayer says.
"With all the monetary stimulus provided to our economy, people worry we'll come to the point where
inflation comes back," he says. "But we may not see that kind of environment for several more years."

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: Beyond Bonds: A Guide to Alternative Income Sources
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: Beyond Bonds: A Guide to Alternative Income Sources
By Mark Hulbert
923 words
11 January 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Attractive alternatives to traditional bonds are hard to find.
But a group of top-performing advisers interviewed for this column nevertheless came up with several --
among them real-estate investment trusts, utilities, energy firms and business-development companies.
The search for bond alternatives is urgent. Interest rates almost certainly will rise this year as the Federal
Reserve continues scaling back its massive stimulus program -- and bond prices fall as interest rates rise.
Even if you intend to hold your bonds until they mature, their current yields are so low that you are virtually
guaranteed to lose ground to inflation.
Nevertheless, many investors are unwittingly increasing their bond holdings this month rather than reducing
them. That is because, every January, they engage in portfolio rebalancing -- selling winners and buying
losers to bring their allocations back into line with what they were a year earlier.
Since stocks gained in 2013 and bonds fell, this rebalancing process could well lead to buying more bonds.
Yet the answer surely isn't to avoid rebalancing altogether, since that would mean maintaining an outsize
allocation to stocks. What, then, should you do with the proceeds realized by reducing your equity holdings to
the desired level?
That is the question posed by this column to a half dozen of the advisers with the best records over the past
15 years, according to the Hulbert Financial Digest. The ranking was based on the Sharpe ratio, which is
calculated by dividing how much money each adviser makes by the volatility his portfolio experienced. By
focusing on performance over 15 years and on risk-adjusted rather than raw returns, we eliminate advisers
whose good records are due primarily to luck.
In first place is Stephen McKee, editor of an advisory service called No Load Mutual Fund Selections &
Timing. He recommends that a portion of the money that would otherwise get rebalanced into bonds instead
be invested in MCG Capital, a business-development company that currently yields more than 11%. (Such
companies invest in smaller enterprises in the initial stages of their development.) A company with a similar
business model that Mr. McKee also likes is MVC Capital, with a 4.0% dividend yield.
"Assuming we are not in a recession and not heading for one, then these business-development companies
look like a pretty good alternative to bonds," Mr. McKee says.
In second place for 15-year risk-adjusted performance is Roger Conrad, editor of Conrad's Utility Investor. His
recommendations: AGL Energy, an Australian utility that yields 4.2%; RioCan Real Estate Investment Trust, a
Canadian REIT with a dividend yield of 5.9%; and Kinder Morgan Energy Partners, a master limited
partnership with a 6.6% yield. (REITs invest in properties, while MLPs typically invest in pipeline, energy and
commodities companies.)
In third place for 15-year performance is Gary Cardiff, editor of the Sound Advice service. He recommends
four REITs that he says are trading at significant discounts to their per-share net asset values: Associated
Estates Realty, CommonWealth REIT, Hersha Hospitality Trust and Retail Opportunity Investments. These
four REITs' average yield is 4.4%.
REITs might seem an inappropriate bond substitute, since they are themselves quite interest-rate sensitive.
But, Mr. Cardiff argues, "while rising interest rates is a near-term negative for real-estate stocks, rising rental
rates from an improving economy should more than compensate in the longer run." Mr. Conrad considers
REITs' selloff last year -- occasioned by a fear of higher rates -- to be a "buying opportunity."
None of the top performers interviewed for this column recommended completely selling all bonds, though
they did advise keeping maturities short to reduce vulnerability to rising interest rates. One of them -- Bob

--- Page 73 ---
Page 73 of 192 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Brinker, editor of Bob Brinker's Marketimer -- recommends that the average duration of your bond holdings be
no longer than 13 months. ("Duration" is a measure of sensitivity to interest-rate fluctuations; a lower duration
implies less sensitivity.)
The four short-term bond funds he recommends are the DoubleLine Low Duration Fund, with a 0.72%
expense ratio, or $72 per $10,000 invested; the Fidelity Floating Rate High Income Fund, with a 0.7% fee; the
Metro West Low Duration Bond Fund, with a 0.57% expense ratio; and the Osterweis Strategic Income Fund,
with a 0.91% fee. Their average current yield is 2.1%.
Mr. Conrad's recommendation for the bond portion of your portfolio: the iShares Utilities Bond
exchange-traded fund, which charges annual fees of 0.3% and has a yield of 3.5%.
Several top advisers also put in a good word for cash and cash alternatives such as money-market funds and
certificates of deposit.
That might look like a no-win solution, concedes James Stack, editor of InvesTech Research Portfolio
Strategy, until "you recognize that bond yields are most likely to rise and the stock market is likely to correct,
which in effect means cash will become more valuable over this coming year relative to both stocks and
bonds."
What about commodities, such as gold and other precious metals? None of the half-dozen top performers
over the past 15 years recommends that you rebalance large amounts into them. That is because their prices
are so volatile that they wouldn't be appropriate substitutes for the bond portion of your portfolio.

mark.hulbert@dowjones.com

Blue-chip stocks retreated for the second time in three sessions, even as more good news on the U.S. job
market helped lift shares closely tied to the economy.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average closed down 68.20 points, or 0.4%, to 16462.74, after rallying 106 points

Wednesday brought a better-than-expected report on private-sector job growth as Automatic Data Processing
and Moody's Analytics said December job growth increased to 238,000. That topped forecasts for gains of
200,000. The ADP report is seen as a preview of the U.S. government 's job report, due Friday.
The materials, financials and information-technology sectors, seen as closely tied to economic growth, were
among the session's winners.
Still, stocks have broadly declined from their most recent peak, set on the final day of 2013, despite a handful
of stronger-than-expected economic reports. But investors say they aren't surprised the market would cool off
after a 30% gain in the S&P 500 last year.
"It stands to reason that stocks weren't going to shoot out of the blocks at the same trajectory as we saw
equities rise in the fourth quarter of last year," said Mark Luschini, chief investment strategist with Janney
Montgomery Scott . "That pace was unsustainable."
Investors Wednesday showed little reaction to the minutes from December's meeting of the Federal
Reserve's decision-making committee, at which it had decided to cut back on its bond-buying program. The
minutes showed that most officials had backed an initial paring of the Fed's stimulus efforts, though some
worried that the recovery might be too fragile.
Fed officials "have been pretty transparent, and nothing was a big surprise, so the markets didn't have to
react," said Ron Florance, deputy chief investment officer for Wells Fargo Private Bank, which manages $170
billion. "They've been clear they're anticipating attractive growth."
Treasury bond prices ticked lower, pushing up yields. The yield on the 10-year note rose to 2.991%, from
2.937% on Tuesday. Yields remain near their highest levels since July 2011. Bond yields move inversely to
prices.
Crude oil lost 1.4%, to settle at $92.33 a barrel. Gold extended losses. The gold futures market had settled
before the Fed release, with the yellow metal falling 0.3%, to $1,225.30 a troy ounce. After release of the
minutes, gold tacked on more losses.
The dollar strengthened slightly against the euro and yen. Currency traders read the minutes as a
reaffirmation of the Fed's commitment to wind down stimulus measures that in the past had weighed on the
dollar.
In Asia early Thursday, the Nikkei was down 1.3%, the Shanghai composite was flat, Hong Kong's Hang
Seng index was down 0.2% and South Korea's Kospi was up 0.1%.
